Geologic history of the San Andreas fault is oversimplified, thus not correct. The fault was created by subduction of the spreading ridge between the Farallon Plate and the Pacific Plate. The Farallon Plate subducted beneath the North American Plate, but the Pacific Plate moved differently. Really interesting stuff to Google if you want to understand how the western US formed. Plate tectonics is very cool!

17:30 creek jogged, but only 300 feet in 100 million years?  Nonsense.  The picket fence moved 8 feet in 1906.  19:00 the movement averaging X inches per year is also nonsense; it moves in spurts, not gradually.  The plate edges stay locked, for Z years or centuries, then break loose.  Hollister shows what the movement TRIES to be.  In other places it fails to move gradually because of shear that can't happen due to the cross-fault grabbing between one side and the other, some of it certainly because of the shapes of the rock interface across the fault line.  The HOLD, but can only hold so much, before failing and slipping/shearing.  The testing at the last part was very good work being done.  Materials science dictates that they test this in the lab, to get info about how much, how far, how strong, etc.  If such data is gotten, then predictions can be improved by magnitudes.

Reading the comments here reminded me of a kind of surreal moment I had one day, in the high desert canyons, on the very North edge of the Grand Canyon. (or, just North of what used to be the Grand Canyon, but is now Lake Powell. Big Water, Utah)  As I was out on my daily hike thru the slot canyons, out in the middle of nowhere, I found myself scrambling up on top of a mesa shelf. So, there I was, surrounded by hundreds of miles of endless canyons, on the border of Arizona and Utah, miles away from any ocean, for sure! And as I looked around at my surroundings, I noticed something that at first my brain couldn't make any sense of. Seashells. Thousands upon thousands of little bright, bleached white, seashells. On top of a mesa, in the middle of the high desert, in the middle of nowhere.  I picked up a handful of them, still trying to process what I was seeing, and holding in my hand, all of these white seashells, carpeting the top of this mesa. I start thinking: seashells? Wait....what??? Why HERE??? HOW?? And finally my brain started to catch up with my surroundings. I realized that all of these little mini, white, clamshell looking seashells had been here, right where I was standing, for a period of time beyond my comprehension. Just laying here, unseen by any human..... for hundreds....of thousands..... of YEARS! They'd been there so long, that they weren't exactly 'shell' any longer. They'd become fossilized, out there in the open air and high desert weather, for eons, and become something that felt like ....almost like chalk, but a bit firmer. I could scratch them easily with my fingernail, and if I put a bit of pressure on them, I could snap of little pieces of them, also like chalk would break. It was mind boggling that they somehow managed to STILL be there, as small and fragile as they were, out in the open air, for such a long time, and somehow managed to still be completely intact and perfect, as calcified and fragile as they were. Incredible!  But, there they were, everywhere! They'd ended up there....when the oceans receded. From the part of the continent that is now the American Southwest. Arizona, Utah, Colorado. ALL of it, had at one time, been UNDER the OCEAN. It really blew my mind! Thinking this dry, barren landscape, had once been deep, deep, deep under the freaking ocean! Wow! It's a memory I will never forget, and will never cease to be amazed about.

Let me add something to think about. I live in Ridgecrest where on July 4th and 5th of 2019, we experienced three earthquakes. A 6.4, 5.4, and 7.1. Living in the desert, I was around for the 1999 Hector Mine event, a 7.0. And in 1992 the 7.3 Landers earthquake.   While the San Andreas fault is fascinating, and capable of a destructive earthquake, it may not happen in our lifetime or even in the next 100 years.   I have lived in California my entire life; 57 years. It is my experience that you are more likely to be affected by a local earthquake. Some of these pack quite a punch and can be quite devastating to local communities. Just look at what happened to Ridgecrest, Landers, Petrolia, Chalfant, Morgan Hill, Coalinga, Tehachapi- just to name a few.   It certainly doesn't hurt to prepare for "The Big One" but don't put all your eggs in one basket. If a smaller, local earthquake hits your community, it may be more than your local emergency services can handle. It can take some time before additional help arrives. That leaves you and your neighbors to help each other. Be prepared to roll up your sleeves and help where you can. Its amazing how a community comes together when disaster strikes. Also be prepared for news crews to take over your town shoving cameras and microphones in your face while there is work to be done.

Hollywood style fear mongering.  In modern times, 2 strong earthquakes have struck Los Angeles.  The 6.6 1971 Sylmar/San Fernando EQ and the 6.7 1994 Northridge EQ were both on the southern flank of the Transverse Ranges which bifurcate Los Angeles County.  The San Andreas Fault Zone (SAFZ) runs along the northern flank of the Transverse Ranges in LA Co.  In the Nineties, a series of 7+ earthquakes struck in the Mojave Desert, northeast of the LA Co. region; the 7.3 1992 Landers EQ in San Bernardino Co. and the 7.1 1999 Hector Mine EQ in the Twenty-Nine Palms area of San Bernardino Co.  In 2019, the 7.1 Ridgecrest EQ struck in the China Lake area of Kern Co.  Most of California's 7+ earthquakes of the past 170 years have been along that same trend, not along the SAFZ.  Also, its the SF Bay area segments of the SAFZ, where proximity provides much greater portent.  The main SAFZ branch spawned the 6.9 1989 Loma Prieta EQ, with its epicenter on the SW flank of  San Jose.  The 7.9 1906 San Francisco EQ actually had its epicenter in nearby Daly City, where the SAFZ goes offshore.  The main SAFZ runs under Stanford University's Linear Accelerator, and underlies the center of the SF Peninsula.  The Hayward Fault, the central splay of the SAFZ, along the eastern shore of SF Bay, bifurcates UC Berkeley's football stadium.  Notoriously, the next Big One is forecast for that stretch of the Hayward Fault.  Los Angeles is buffered by 10,000-ft mountain range between it and the nearest stretch of the SAFZ.  But such details don't matter in Hollywood, which is a district of Los Angeles.

Geologists have found out that a lot of movement in one spot quite often , and a lot of movement perhaps 10's to 100's of miles away on the same fault line is quite normal as the built-up pressures are adjusting. This movement is quite common but in the areas in between get hung up on asperites that makes that section(s) harder to move.  The problem with this is that the pressures continue to build up even though there's no movement. When it breaks loose, the movement can be catastrophic. There are several other faults in California that these pressures are built-up waiting to snap.

115 AD.
I was a miner working in the tunnel, for piping water from Northern California, to Los Angeles. We were about six miles in, of 12 miles, we hit very soft earth, our tunnel caved in, it took about two weeks to shore it up so, we could get back on track. I found myself standing in wet sand, I got down for a closer look, I found sand that was once a beach, there was even sea shells there that looked like they were picked up off the beach that day. For some reason we were following the Andreas fault. It made me realize how fresh the mountains were shaped. I took some of the sea shells home in my lunchbox. They were so ordinary, people wouldnâ€™t have believed where they came from.

Berkland (July 31, 1930 â€“ July 22, 2016) was an American geologist who controversially claimed to be able to predict earthquakes, including the 1989 Loma Prieta earthquake and 1994 Northridge Earthquake and who popularized the idea that some people are earthquake sensitive. Snow storms in New England often cause more casualties than hurricanes. The Great New Madrid Earthquakes in 1811 and 1812 hit the Midwestern USA. The series of intraplate earthquakes covered an area 10 times greater than the 1906 San Francisco Earthquake.  And the Moment Magnitude of one of the quakes was estimated to be 8.2. Another interesting note, as all animals with legs strait down are migratory, unlike legs pointing out like alligator and turtle, so when the earth spread and cracked and water rushed in, those migratory pathways became blocked.  Those animals were trapped, and ended, or were isolated on the chunk of earth that broke away.  Today, we find animals on islands that have been isolated for millions of years and never seen anywhere else on earth. @15:21 "It's molecular structure means that..." No. This is a mistake. It's NUCLEIC structure means that, i.e. having too many neutrons in the nucleus causes ATOMIC instability, not molecular instability--except of course for in that final moment when one of its extra neutrons finally turns into a proton, and it becomes Nitrogen 14 instead. There is virtually no effect at all on its molecular structure until this final moment, and so it behaves like any other carbon inside our cells, forming the exact same molecules, and only rarely turning into nitrogen and probably causing terrible damage in whatever cell it was in when that happens. From the timing of previous quakes, I noticed that there is a correlation with LAND TIDES (from the moon) that might trigger highly stressed areas.  All they may need is that little extra "bump" to set them off.  While Earthquakes cannot be predicted, The danger might be especially high when the land tide put extra stress on already stressed areas.  The least the cities could do is make sure fire trucks and emergency vehicles are parked out of doors and other preventive emergency measures taken when the area is to be affected by land tides.       In the 1971 quake I noticed it happened the night of a FULL moon and lunar eclipse at a time where tidal forces peaked(Around 6 am)  That inspired me to check moon position on previous quakes.---  I went back and noticed the tidal forces(no eclipse then) were also at maximum (from moon position) in the quake that happened in the 1930s  Also there was a tidal correlation with the San Francisco quake in 1906---         Periods of maximum compression or tension from Land tides might indeed be a trigger in many cases of earthquakes in California.   Sorry ,I have not followed up, but this should be looked into. (I am a Geologist, BTW)

My boyfriend and I went on vacation last summer. We made a big loop which went as far northwest as Yellowstone, down through Colorado, to Arizona to see the Grand Canyon then worked our way back home to Missouri just outside of St Louis. I'd gone to all these places as well kid with my parents. I've traveled to Colorado alot since my brother, nieces and nephews live there. This time, though we experienced a bizarre phenomenon on ALL THE HIGHWAYS starting when heading southeast from Yellowstone. Colorado and Arizona  were the worst. There were these sudden sharp creases in the pavement--where the Highway bulged upward creating a ramp. It sent our 3/4 ton pickup and 40 foot travel trailer airborne. These "ramps" occurred repeatedly. They were a mile apart or so on average. Even when we slowed  down below the speed limit we still went airborne. This happened so many times it broke our exhaust system lose 3 times!!! When we asked about the roads, we were told by people that it was due to geological activity. This was NOT THE WAY THE ROADS WERE WHEN I WAS YOUNG. So something IS going on. It may be hundreds of years before something significant occurs since the geologic process is slow compared to the way humanity keeps time. But who knows? Could be sooner than we think. Just saying.

The one thing southern Californian's may not be aware of is out by Palm Springs there are two major transportation corridors located in that area,  Interstate 10, and the Union Pacific Yuma Subdivision. Both carry transportation in and out of that region. The Union Pacific Yuma Subdivision is along a rail corridor called the Sunset Route. Amtrak's Sunset Limited and the Texas Eagle runs that subdivision into West Colton Yard in Colton CA.   Northwest of there lies the San Bernardino Mountains that sits on the North American Plate, and the San Gabriel Mountains that sits on the Pacific Plate. Between the two mountain ranges sits Cajon Pass. This is where a part of the San Andreas Fault . In this pass sits Interstate 15, Historic Route 66, the BNSF Southern Transcon, and the Union Pacific Mojave Subdivision. The importance of this pass cannot be calculated. The BNSF Southern Transcon and where it runs through the pass is called the Cajon Subdivision. Amtrak's Southwest Chief runs this subdivision. The BNSF Southern Transcon runs from Chicago to the Ports of Long Beach and Los Angeles, and is responsible for about 25 to 30% of the country's rail freight. The transcon runs through the states of Illinois, Iowa, Missouri, Kansas, Oklahoma, Texas, New Mexico,  Arizona, and California respectively.   I-15 which is a major trucking and automotive transportation corridor that connects with I-40 in Barstow, I-80 in Salt Lake City, and I-90 in Butte Montana. Northwest of the Cajon Pass lies Interstate 5 near Gorman. The fault slices through that area as well. I-5 begins on the US/Canadian border north of Blaine Washington, and ends at the US/Mexican border south of San Diego. This too is an important corridor. This connects  I-90 in Seattle, I-84 in Portland Oregon, I-80 in Sacramento, I-10 in Los Angeles, I-15 in San Diego.
